Cypria

History of the Creation of the World

Prologue
    Call to the Muses
      O Muses! sing to us of the formation of the world. O Muses! cantabo nobis inhaerere in educatione administrorum mundi. O Clio! tell us the history of the world in the time of the Old Gods. O Clio! dic nobis in Vetus historia mundi et in tempore Deorum. O Calliope! let your words flow onto this page true and honorable. O Calliope! verba tua influunt onto hanc paginam vera et honesta.
      Helicon
      A mountain of Boeotia, Greece, sacred to Apollo and the Muses
      Permessus
      River of Boeotia, whose springs were situated on the Helicon
      Olmeius
      Stream in Mount Helicon that runs into Permessus
      Argos
      Ancient city in Greece

      á¼ˆÎ»Î»á½° but Ï„á½· á¼¦ Î¼Î¿Î¹ Ï„Î±á¿¦Ï„Î± Ï€ÎµÏá½¶ Î´Ïá¿¦Î½ á¼¢ Ï€ÎµÏá½¶ Ï€á½³Ï„ÏÎ·Î½; 35       Come thou, let us begin with the Muses who gladden the great spirit of their father Jupiter in Olympus with their songs, telling of things that are and that shall be and that were aforetime with consenting voice. Unwearying flows the sweet sound from their lips, and the house of their father Jupiter the loud-thunderer is glad at the lily-like voice of the goddesses as it spread abroad, and the peaks of snowy Olympus resound, and the homes of the immortals. And they uttering their immortal voice, celebrate in song first of all the reverend race of the gods from the beginning, those whom Earth and wide Heaven begot, and the gods sprung of these, givers of good things. Then, next, the goddesses sing of Jupiter, the father of gods and men, as they begin and end their strain, how much he is the most excellent among the gods and supreme in power. And again, they chant the race of men and strong giants, and gladden the heart of Jupiter within Olympus, â€” the Olympian Muses, daughters of Jupiter the aegis-holder.       Charites
      The goddesses of charm, beauty, nature, human creativity, and fertility, and the patronesses of amusement and festivities; the retinue of Venus
      For nine nights did wise Jupiter lie with Her, entering Her holy bed remote from the immortals. And when a year was passed and the seasons came round as the months waned, and many days were accomplished, She bare nine Daughters, all of one mind, whose hearts are set upon song and Their spirit free from care, a little way from the topmost peak of snowy Olympus. There are Their bright dancing-places and beautiful homes, and beside Them the Charites and Himerus Desire live in delight. And They, uttering through Their lips a lovely voice, sing the laws of all and the goodly ways of the immortals, uttering their lovely voice. Then went They to Olympus, delighting in their sweet voice, with heavenly song, and the dark earth resounded about Them as They chanted, and a lovely sound rose up beneath Their feet as They went to Their Father. And He was reigning in heaven, Himself holding the lightning and glowing thunderbolt, when He had overcome by might His Father Saturn; and He distributed fairly to the immortals Their portions and declared Their privileges. á¼˜Î½Î½á½³Î± nine Î³á½±Ï Î¿á¼± Î½Ï…ÎºÏ„á½¸Ï‚ genitive night á¼Î¼á½·ÏƒÎ³ÎµÏ„Î¿ Î¼Î·Ï„á½·ÎµÏ„Î± counselor; all-wise one Î–Îµá½ºÏ‚ Jupiter

      á¼€Î¸Î±Î½á½±Ï„Î¿Î¹Ï‚ undying, immortal Î´Î¹á½³Ï„Î±Î¾ÎµÎ½ á½Î¼á¿¶Ï‚ together; equally, alike ÎºÎ±á½¶ and á¼Ï€á½³Ï†ÏÎ±Î´Îµ think of doing Ï„Î¹Î¼á½±Ï‚.       These things, then, the Muses sang who dwell on Olympus, nine daughters begotten by great Jupiter, Cleio and Euterpe, Thaleia, Melpomene and Terpsichore, and Erato and Polyhymnia and Urania and Calliope (3), who is the chiefest of them all, for she attends on worshipful princes: whomsoever of heaven-nourished princes the daughters of great Jupiter honour, and behold him at his birth, they pour sweet dew upon his tongue, and from his lips flow gracious words. All the people look towards him while he settles causes with true judgements: and he, speaking surely, would soon make wise end even of a great quarrel; for therefore are there princes wise in heart, because when the people are being misguided in their assembly, they set right the matter again with ease, persuading them with gentle words. And when he passes through a gathering, they greet him as a god with gentle reverence, and he is conspicuous amongst the assembled: such is the holy gift of the Muses to men. For it is through the Muses and far-shooting Apollo that there are singers and harpers upon the earth; but princes are of Jupiter, and happy is he whom the Muses love: sweet flows speech from his mouth. For though a man have sorrow and grief in his newly-troubled soul and live in dread because his heart is distressed, yet, when a singer, the servant of the Muses, chants the glorious deeds of men of old and the blessed gods who inhabit Olympus, at once he forgets his heaviness and remembers not his sorrows at all; but the gifts of the goddesses soon turn him away from these. Grant lovely song and celebrate the holy race of the deathless gods who are for ever, those that were born of Earth and starry Heaven and gloomy Night and them that briny Sea did rear.       Verily at the first Chaos came to be, á¼® Ï„Î¿Î¹ verily; truthfully Î¼á½²Î½ Ï€Ïá½½Ï„Î¹ÏƒÏ„Î± first; foremost Î§á½±Î¿Ï‚ Chaos Î³á½³Î½ÎµÏ„á¾½ came into being, Before the sea and the earth, and the sky which covers all, Ante before mare et terras et quod tegit omnia caelum there was one, the whole of nature looking over the world, unus erat toto naturae vultus in orbe globe, which they called Chaos: a rough and confused mass quem dixere chaos: rudis indigestaque moles and there was nothing but this inert weight not well joined, the discordant seeds of things. nec quicquam nisi pondus iners congestaque eodem
      non bene iunctarum discordia semina rerum.       No sun shone light upon the world, nor the moon's horns; nullus adhuc mundo praebebat lumina Titan Sol,
      nec nova crescendo reparabat cornua Phoebe Titan Goddess of the moon,       nor was the earth floating in the air, nec circumfuso pendebat in aere tellus
      ponderibus librata suis,       nor was a long ocean stretched, nec bracchia longo
      margine terrarum porrexerat Amphitrite,       nor was there land or sea or air. utque erat et tellus illic et pontus et aer, In one body there was cold conflicting against heat, wet against dry, soft against hard, quia corpore in uno
      frigida pugnabant calidis, umentia siccis,
      mollia cum duris,       and that with weight conflicting against that without weight. idque pondere pondus contra opponi. Chaos broke apart this strife. Hanc deus god et melior litem natura diremit. He separated the lands from the sea and the liquid apart from the air. nam caelo terras et terris abscidit undas
      et liquidum spisso secrevit ab aere caelum.       That which He had disengaged and extricated from the blind heap, He bound disjoined in their places in concordant peace: quae postquam evolvit caecoque exemit acervo,
      dissociata locis concordi pace ligavit:       The fiery force of the heavens bowed outward and that without weight shot forth and chose for itself a place in the highest citadel; ignea convexi vis et sine pondere caeli
      emicuit summaque locum sibi fecit in arce;       The air is next to it in lightness and in place; proximus est aer illi levitate locoque; the earth is denser than these and drew with it the gross elements and was pressed by gravity into itself; densior his tellus elementaque grandia traxit
      et pressa est gravitate sua;

    Dividing the Universe into the Separate RealmsWhen He had divided the heap thus arranged, He had reduced it divided into members: Sic ubi dispositam congeriem secuit sectamque in membra coegit, First He collected the land into the form of a great globe, lest it might not be equal in every part. principio terram, ne non aequalis ab omni parte foret, magni speciem glomeravit in orbis.

    The Mediterranean Sea
    She bare also the fruitless deep with His raging swell, Pontus, without sweet union of love. á¼­ who Î´á½² postpositional but ÎºÎ±á½¶ and á¼€Ï„Ïá½»Î³ÎµÏ„Î¿Î½ prepare Ï€á½³Î»Î±Î³Î¿Ï‚ sea Ï„á½³ÎºÎµÎ½ gave birth, Î¿á¼´Î´Î¼Î±Ï„Î¹ to hope for Î¸Ï…á¿–Î¿Î½ rage,
    Î á½¹Î½Ï„Î¿Î½ Pontus; the Mediterranean Sea, á¼„Ï„ÎµÏ without Ï†Î¹Î»á½¹Ï„Î·Ï„Î¿Ï‚ intercourse á¼Ï†Î¹Î¼á½³ÏÎ¿Ï… genitive longed for, desired, charming.     Chaos ordered the seas to be poured abroad and to swell with the rapid winds and to surround the shores of the compassed earth. tum freta diffundi rapidisque tumescere ventis
    iussit et ambitae circumdare litora terrae;     He added also fountains, and immense pools and lakes; addidit et fontes et stagna inmensa lacusque And He girt with sloping banks the descending rivers, which, different in their places, are partially absorbed by the earth itself, fluminaque obliquis cinxit declivia ripis,
    quae, diversa locis, partim sorbentur ab ipsa,     and others arrive into the seas and, being received in the plain of freer water, beat shores instead of banks. in mare perveniunt partim campoque recepta
    liberioris aquae pro ripis litora pulsant.     He ordered also plains to be stretch out, valleys to sink down, woods to be covered with foliage, and stoney mountains to rise, iussit et extendi campos, subsidere valles,
    fronde tegi silvas, lapidosos surgere montes,
    Mountains
    ÎŸá½”ÏÎµÎ±Ourea
    Montes
    and She brought forth long Mountains, graceful haunts of the Goddess-Nymphs who dwell amongst the glens of the hills; Î“Îµá½·Î½Î±Ï„Î¿ She bore Î´á¾½ postpositional but ÎŸá½”ÏÎµÎ± mountains Î¼Î±ÎºÏá½± distant, Î¸Îµá¿¶Î½ genitive Gods Ï‡Î±Ïá½·ÎµÎ½Ï„Î±Ï‚ graceful, beautiful á¼Î½Î±á½»Î»Î¿Ï…Ï‚,
    ÎÏ…Î¼Ï†á½³Ï‰Î½ Nymphs Î±á¼³ f plural who Î½Î±á½·Î¿Ï…ÏƒÎ¹Î½ dwell á¼€Î½á¾½ ÎŸá½”ÏÎµÎ± mountains Î²Î·ÏƒÏƒá½µÎµÎ½Ï„Î±;     And as two zones He cut the sky on the right part, and as many on the left, and a fifth part hotter than those, utque duae dextra caelum totidemque sinistra
    parte secant zonae, quinta est ardentior illis,     so the care of God distinguished by the same number the included mass, and as many climes are marked out of the earth: sic onus inclusum numero distinxit eodem
    cura dei, totidemque plagae tellure land, country premuntur.     Of which that which is in the middle is not habitable from heat; Deep snow covers two others quarum quae media est, non est habitabilis aestu;
    nix tegit alta duas;     He placed as many between these climes and gave them temperateness, flame being mixed with cold. totidem inter utramque locavit
    temperiemque dedit mixta cum frigore flamma.     Over these impends the air, which is by so much heavier than fire, as the weight of water is lighter than the weight of the earth. Inminet his aer, qui, quanto est pondere terrae
    pondus aquae levius, tanto est onerosior igni.     And there He ordered vapors, there clouds to collect, and thunders about to move human minds, and with thunderbolts winds producing cold. illic et nebulas, illic consistere nubes
    iussit et humanas motura tonitrua mentes
    et cum fulminibus facientes fulgura ventos.     The maker of the world did not permit these winds to take whatever part of the air they chose; His quoque non passim mundi fabricator habendum
    aera permisit;     Even now, since each directs his own blasts in a different tract, they are barely kept from tearing the world apart, such is the discord amongst the brothers. vix nunc obsistitur illis,
    cum sua quisque regat diverso flamina tractu,
    quin lanient mundum; tanta est discordia fratrum.
    Nabataean kingdoms
    Arabic kingdom centred in Petra that fell to Rome in 859 AUC. They were central in the trade of frankincense and myrrh. Land today in southern Jordan and southern Israel.
    Persis
    Persia proper, a region in southern Iran/Persia.
    Eurus the east wind retired to the rising morn and the Nabataean kingdoms, and Persis, the hilltops lying under the morning rays; Eurus ad Auroram Nabataeaque regna recessit,
    Persidaque et radiis iuga subdita matutinis;     The evening star and shores which are warmed by the setting sun, are nearest to Zephyr the west wind; vesper et occiduo quae litora sole tepescunt,
    proxima sunt Zephyro;
    Scythia
    The steppes of Central Eurasia north of the Black Sea; modern day Ukraine, south Russia, and Kazakhstan.
    And the freezing Boreas the north wind invaded Scythia and the north; Scythiam septemque triones
    horrifer invasit Boreas;     The opposite land to the south grows wet by constant clouds and rainy Auster the south wind. contraria tellus nubibus adsiduis pluviaque madescit ab Austro. Over these He placed Aether, liquid and void of weight, nor having aught of earthly dregs, haec super inposuit liquidum et gravitate carentem
    aethera nec quicquam terrenae faecis habentem,     and to forever be the secure seats of the blessed Gods. á½„Ï†Ïá¾½ in order to Îµá¼´Î· may go Î¼Î±Îºá½±ÏÎµÏƒÏƒÎ¹ blessed Î¸ÎµÎ¿á¿–Ï‚ Gods á¼•Î´Î¿Ï‚ seat á¼€ÏƒÏ†Î±Î»á½²Ï‚ secure Î±á¼°Îµá½· forever.
    Creating Animals and Man
      Scarcely had He separated all these within certain limits, when the stars, which had lay hidden under that mass, began to blaze out in the whole sky. Vix ita limitibus dissaepserat omnia certis,
      cum, quae pressa diu fuerant caligine caeca,
      sidera coeperunt toto effervescere caelo;       And lest any region might be destitute of its own animals, the stars and the forms of Gods hold the celestial floor; neu regio foret ulla suis animalibus orba,
      astra tenent caeleste solum formaeque deorum,       the waters gave way to be inhabited by glittering fishes; cesserunt nitidis habitandae piscibus undae, the land received wild beasts, and the air the flying birds. terra feras cepit, volucres agitabilis aer. An animal more sacred than these, and more capable of higher thought, and might be able to rule of the rest, was still wanting. Sanctius his animal mentisque capacius altae
      deerat adhuc et quod dominari in cetera posset:       Man was born; whether that maker of all things, the origin of a better world, made him from divine seed, natus homo est, sive hunc divino semine fecit
      ille opifex rerum, mundi melioris origo,       or Earth, still new and lately severed from the lofty aether, retained seeds of the kindred Sky, sive recens tellus seductaque nuper ab alto
      aethere cognati retinebat semina caeli,       which the Son of Iapetus Prometheus mixed with river waters and fashioned into the image of the Gods who control all things. quam satus Iapeto, mixtam pluvialibus undis,
      finxit in effigiem moderantum cuncta deorum,       The other animals groveling look at earth, He gave to man a lofty aspect and ordered him to view the sky and to raise his uplifted looks to the stars. pronaque cum spectent animalia cetera terram,
      os homini sublime dedit caelumque videre
      iussit et erectos ad sidera tollere vultus:       Thus, the earth which had been just now rude and without shape, being changed, put on the unknown figures of men. sic, modo quae fuerat rudis et sine imagine, tellus
      induit ignotas hominum conversa figuras.       This is how Chaos brought order; and then He retired and He left behind Mother Earth and Father Heaven. Hoc est quam Chaos protulit ordo; et inde abscessit
      et reliquit Caelus Pater et Terra Mater.

    Enuma Elish â€” Prometheus kills Abzu
      The divine Brothers came together, Their clamour got loud, throwing Tiamat into a turmoil. They jarred the nerves of Tiamat,
      Anduruna
      â€˜Heavenly abodeâ€™, probably refers to the home of the Gods
      And by Their dancing They spread alarm in Anduruna. Abzu did not diminish Their clamour, And Tiamat was silent when confronted with Them. Their conduct was displeasing to Her, Yet though Their behaviour was not good, She wished to spare Them. Thereupon Abzu, the begetter of the great Gods, called Mummu, His vizier, and addressed him, "Vizier Mummu, who gratifies My pleasure, "Come, let Us go to Tiamat!" They went and sat, facing Tiamat, As They conferred about the Gods, Their Sons. Abzu opened His mouth And addressed Tiamat "Their behaviour has become displeasing to Me "And I cannot rest in the day-time or sleep at night. "I will destroy and break up Their way of life "That silence may reign and We may sleep." When Tiamat heard this She raged and cried out to Her Spouse, She cried in distress, fuming within Herself, She grieved over the (plotted) evil, "How can We destroy what We have given birth to? "Though Their behaviour causes distress, let Us tighten discipline graciously." Mummu spoke up with counsel for Abzuâ€” (As from) a rebellious vizier was the counsel of His Mummuâ€” "Destroy, my Father, that lawless way of life, "That you may rest in the day-time and sleep by night!" Abzu was pleased with him, His face beamed Because he had plotted evil against the Gods, His Sons. Mummu put his arms around Abzu's neck, He sat on His knees kissing Him. What They plotted in Their Gathering Was reported to the Gods, Their Sons. The Gods heard it and were frantic. They were overcome with silence and sat quietly. Prometheus, who excels in knowledge, the skilled and learned, Prometheus, who knows everything, perceived Their tricks. He fashioned it and made it to be all-embracing, He executed it skilfully as supremeâ€”His pure incantation. He recited it and set it on the waters, He poured sleep upon Him as He was slumbering deeply. He put Abzu to slumber as He poured out sleep, And Mummu, the counsellor, was breathless with agitation. He split Abzu's sinews, ripped off his crown, Carried away His aura and put it on himself. He bound Abzu and killed him; Mummu He confined and handled roughly. He set his dwelling upon Abzu, And laid hold on Mummu, keeping the nose-rope in His hand. After Prometheus had bound and slain His enemies, Had achieved victory over His foes, He rested quietly in His chamber, He called it Abzu, whose shrines He appointed. Then He founded His living-quarters within it,

      Enuma Elish, Tablet I
        Anu formed and gave birth to the four winds, He delivered Them to Him, "My son, let them whirl!" He formed dust and set a hurricane to drive it, He made a wave to bring consternation on Tiamat. Tiamat was confounded; day and night she was frantic. The Gods took no rest, They . . . . . . . In Their minds They plotted evil, And addressed Their mother Tiamat, "When ApsÃ», Your spouse, was killed, You did not go at his side, but sat quietly. The four dreadful winds have been fashioned To throw You into confusion, and We cannot sleep. You gave no thought to ApsÃ», Your spouse, Nor to Mummu, who is a prisoner. Now You sit alone. Henceforth You will be in frantic consternation! And as for Us, who cannot rest, You do not love us! Consider Our burden, Our eyes are hollow. Break the immovable yoke that We may sleep. Make battle, avenge Them! [ . . ] . . . . reduce to nothingness!" Tiamat heard, the speech pleased Her, (She said,) "Let Us make demons, [as You] have advised." The Gods assembled within Her. They conceived [evil] against the Gods Their begetters. They . . . . . and took the side of Tiamat, Fiercely plotting, unresting by night and day, Lusting for battle, raging, storming, They set up a host to bring about conflict. Mother Hubur, who forms everything, Supplied irresistible weapons, and gave birth to giant serpents. They had sharp teeth, they were merciless . . . . With poison instead of blood she filled their bodies. She clothed the fearful monsters with dread, She loaded them with an aura and made them godlike. (She said,) "Let their onlooker feebly perish, May they constantly leap forward and never retire." She created the Hydra, the Dragon, the Hairy Hero The Great Demon, the Savage Dog, and the Scorpion-man, Fierce demons, the Fish-man, and the Bull-man, Carriers of merciless weapons, fearless in the face of battle. Her commands were tremendous, not to be resisted. Altogether she made eleven of that kind.
        Among the gods, her sons, whom she constituted her host, She exalted Kingu, and magnified him among them. The leadership of the army, the direction of the host, The bearing of weapons, campaigning, the mobilization of conflict, The chief executive power of battle, supreme command, She entrusted to him and set him on a throne, "I have cast the spell for you and exalted you in the host of the gods, I have delivered to you the rule of all the gods. You are indeed exalted, my spouse, you are renowned, Let your commands prevail over all the Anunnaki." She gave him the Tablet of Destinies and fastened it to his breast, (Saying) "Your order may not be changed; let the utterance of Your mouth be firm." After Kingu was elevated and had acquired the power of Anuship, He decreed the destinies for the Gods, Her Sons: "May the utterance of your mouths subdue the fire-god, May your poison by its accumulation put down aggression."
      Enuma Elish, Tablet II, lines 1â€“162
        Tiamat gathered together her creation And organised battle against the gods, her offspring. Henceforth Tiamat plotted evil because of ApsÃ» It became known to Ea/Enki that she had arranged the conflict. Ea/Enki heard this matter, He lapsed into silence in his chamber and sat motionless. After he had reflected and his anger had subsided He directed his steps to AnÅ¡ar his father.
        He entered the presence of the father of his begetter, AnÅ¡ar, And related to him all of Tiamat's plotting. "My father, Tiamat our mother has conceived a hatred for us, She has established a host in her savage fury. All the gods have turned to her, Even those you (pl.) begat also take her side They . . . . . and took the side of Tiamat, Fiercely plotting, unresting by night and day, Lusting for battle, raging, storming, They set up a host to bring about conflict. Mother Hubur, who forms everything, Supplied irresistible weapons, and gave birth to giant serpents. They had sharp teeth, they were merciless. With poison instead of blood she filled their bodies. She clothed the fearful monsters with dread, She loaded them with an aura and made them godlike. (She said,) "Let their onlooker feebly perish, May they constantly leap forward and never retire." She created the Hydra, the Dragon, the Hairy Hero, The Great Demon, the Savage Dog, and the Scorpion-man, Fierce demons, the Fish-man, and the Bull-man, Carriers of merciless weapons, fearless in the face of battle. The leadership of the army, the direction of the host, The bearing of weapons, campaigning, the mobilization of conflict, The chief executive power of battle supreme command, She entrusted to him and set him on a throne. "I have cast the spell for you and exalted you in the host of the gods, I have delivered to you the rule of all the gods. You are indeed exalted, my spouse, you are renowned, Let your commands prevail over all the Anunnaki." She gave him the tablet of Destinies and fastened it to his breast, (Saying) "Your order may not he changed; let the utterance of your mouth be firm." After Kingu was elevated and had acquired the power of Anuship He decreed the destinies for the gods, her sons: "May the utterance of your mouths subdue the fire-god, May your poison by its accumulation put down aggression."
        AnÅ¡ar heard; the matter was profoundly disturbing. He cried "Woe!" and bit his lip. His heart was in fury, his mind could not be calmed. Over Ea/Enki his son his cry was faltering. "My son, you who provoked the war, Take responsibility for whatever you alone have done! You set out and killed ApsÃ», And as for Tiamat, whom you made furious, where is her equal?"
        The gatherer of counsel, the learned prince, The creator of wisdom, the god Enki With soothing words and calming utterance Gently answered [his] father AnÅ¡ar "My father, deep mind, who decrees destiny, Who has the power to bring into being and destroy, AnÅ¡ar, deep mind, who decrees destiny, Who has the power to bring into being and to destroy, I want to say something to you, calm down for me for a moment And consider that I performed a helpful deed. Before I killed ApsÃ» Who could have seen the present situation? Before I quickly made an end of him What were the circumstances were I to destroy him?"
        AnÅ¡ar heard, the words pleased him. His heart relaxed to speak to Ea/Enki, "My son, your deeds are fitting for a god, You are capable of a fierce, unequalled blow . . [ . . . ] Ea/Enki, your deeds are fitting for a god, You are capable of a fierce, unequalled blow . . [ . . . ] Go before Tiamat and appease her attack, . . [ . . . ] . . . her fury with [your] incantation."
        He heard the speech of AnÅ¡ar his father, He took the road to her, proceeded on the route to her. He went, he perceived the tricks of Tiamat, [He stopped], fell silent, and turned back. [He] entered the presence of august AnÅ¡ar Penitently addressing him, "[My father], Tiamat's deeds are too much for me. I perceived her planning, and [my] incantation was not equal (to it). Her strength is mighty, she is full of dread, She is altogether very strong, none can go against her. Her very loud cry did not diminish, [I became afraid] of her cry and turned back. [My father], do not lose hope, send a second person against her. Though a woman's strength is very great, it is not equal to a man's. Disband her cohorts, break up her plans Before she lays her hands on us."
        AnÅ¡ar cried out in intense fury, Addressing Anu his son, "Honoured son, hero, warrior, Whose strength is mighty, whose attack is irresistible Hasten and stand before Tiamat, Appease her rage that her heart may relax If she does not harken to your words, Address to her words of petition that she may be appeased."
        He heard the speech of AnÅ¡ar his father, He took the road to her, proceeded on the route to her. Anu went, he perceived the tricks of Tiamat, He stopped, fell silent, and turned back. He entered the presence of AnÅ¡ar the father who begat him, Penitently addressing him. "My father, Tiamat's [deeds] are too much for me. I perceived her planning, but my [incantation] was not [equal] (to it). Her strength is mighty, she is [full] of dread, She is altogether very strong, no one [can go against her]. Her very loud noise does not diminish, I became afraid of her cry and turned back. My father, do not lose hope, send another person against her. Though a woman's strength is very great, it is not equal to a man's. Disband her cohorts, break up her plans, Before she lays her hands on us."
        AnÅ¡ar lapsed into silence, staring at the ground, He nodded to Ea/Enki, shaking his head. The Igigi and all the Anunnaki had assembled, They sat in tight-lipped silence. No god would go to face . . [ . . ] Would go out against Tiamat . . . . [ . . ] Yet the lord AnÅ¡ar, the father of the great gods, Was angry in his heart, and did not summon any one. A mighty son, the avenger of his father, He who hastens to war, the warrior Marduk Ea/Enki summoned (him) to his private chamber To explain to him his plans. "Marduk, give counsel, listen to your father. You are my son, who gives me pleasure, Go reverently before AnÅ¡ar, Speak, take your stand, appease him with your glance." Be-l rejoiced at his father's words, He drew near and stood in the presence of AnÅ¡ar. AnÅ¡ar saw him, his heart filled with satisfaction, He kissed his lips and removed his fear.
        "My [father] do not hold your peace, but speak forth, I will go and fulfil your desires! [AnÅ¡ar,] do not hold your peace, but speak forth, I will go and fulfil your desires! Which man has drawn up his battle array against you? And will Tiamat, who is a woman, attack you with (her) weapons? [My father], begetter, rejoice and be glad, Soon you will tread on the neck of Tiamat! [AnÅ¡ar], begetter, rejoice and be glad, Soon you will tread on the neck of Tiamat!" ["Go,] my son, conversant with all knowledge, Appease Tiamat with your pure spell. Drive the storm chariot without delay, And with a [ . . ] which cannot be repelled turn her back." Be-l rejoiced at his father's words, With glad heart he addressed his father, "Lord of the gods, Destiny of the great gods, If I should become your avenger, If I should bind Tiamat and preserve you, Convene an assembly and proclaim for me an exalted destiny. Sit, all of you, in UpÅ¡ukkinakku with gladness, And let me, with my utterance, decree destinies instead of you. Whatever I instigate must not be changed, Nor may my command be nullified or altered."
      Enuma Elish, Tablet III, lines 1â€“138
        AnÅ¡ar opened his mouth And addressed Kaka, his vizier, "Vizier Kaka, who gratifies my pleasure, I will send you to Lahmu and Lahamu. You are skilled in making inquiry, learned in address. Have the gods, my fathers, brought to my presence. Let all the gods be brought, Let them confer as they sit at table. They sat down in their assembly, summoning the destinies, And with all due rites they called his name:

        (10) Asarre, the giver of arable land who established plough-land, The creator of barley and flax, who made plant life grow.
        (11) Asaralim, who is revered in the counsel chamber, whose counsel excels, The gods heed it and grasp fear of him.
        (12) Asaralimnunna, the noble, the light of the father, his begetter, Who directs the decrees of Anu, Enlil, and Ea/Enki, that is NinÅ¡iku. He is their provisioner, who assigns their incomes, Whose turban multiplies abundance for the land.
        (13) Tutu is he, who accomplishes their renovation, Let him purify their sanctuaries that they may repose. Let him fashion an incantation that the gods may rest, Though they rise up in fury, let them withdraw. He is indeed exalted in the assembly of the gods, his [fathers], No one among the gods can [equal] him.
        (14) Tutu-Ziukkinna, the life of [his] host, Who established, the pure heavens for the gods, Who took charge of their courses, who appointed [their stations], May he not be forgotten among mortals, but [let them remember] his deeds.
        (15) Tutu-Ziku they called him thirdly, the establisher of purification, The god of the pleasant breeze, lord of success and obedience, Who produces bounty and wealth, who establishes abundance, Who turns everything scant that we have into profusion, Whose p1easant breeze we sniffed in time of terrible trouble, Let men command that his praises be constantly uttered, let them offer worship to him.
        As (16) Tutu-Agaku, fourthly, let humans extol him, Lord of the pure incantation, who brought the dead back to life, Who showed mercy on the Bound Gods, Who threw the imposed yoke on the gods, his enemies, And to spare them created mankind. The merciful, in whose power it is to restore to life, Let his words be sure and not forgotten From the mouths of the black-heads, his creatures.
        As (17) Tutu-Tuku, fifthly, let their mouth give expression to his pure spell, Who extirpated all the wicked by his pure incantation.
        (18) Å azu, who knew the heart of the gods, who saw the reins, Who did not let an evil-doer escape from him, Who established the assembly of the gods, who rejoiced their hearts, Who subjugated the disobedient, he is the gods' encompassing protection. He made truth to prosper, he uprooted perverse speech, He separated falsehood from truth.
        As (19) Å azu-Zisi, secondly, let them continually praise him, the subduer of aggressors, Who ousted consternation off from the bodies of the gods, his fathers.
        (20) Å azu-Suhrim, thirdly, who extirpated every foe with his weapons, Who confounded their plans and turned them into wind. He snuffed out all the wicked who came against him, Let the gods ever shout acclamations in the assembly.
        (21) Å azu-Suhgurim, fourthly, who established success for the gods, his fathers, Who extirpated foes and destroyed their offspring, Who scattered their achievements, leaving no part of them, Let his name be spoken and proclaimed in the land.
        As (22) Å azu-Zahrim, fifthly, let future gererations discuss him, The destroyer of every rebel, of all the disobedient, Who brought all the fugitive gods into the shrines, Let this name of his be established.
        As (23) Å azu-Zahgurim, sixthly, let them altogether and everywhere worship him, Who himself destroyed all the foes in battle.
        (24) Enbilulu is he, the lord who supplies them abundantly, Their great chosen one, who provides cereal offerings, Who keeps pasturage and watering in good condition and established it for the land, Who opened watercourses and distributed plentiful water.
        (25) Enbilulu-Epadun, lord of common land and . . ., let them [call him] secondly, Canal supervisor of heaven and netherworld, who sets the furrow, Who establishes clean arable land in the open country, Who directs irrigation ditch and canal, and marks out the furrow.
        As (26) Enbilulu-Gugal, canal supervisor of the water courses of the gods, let them praise him thirdly, Lord of abundance, profusion, and huge stores (of grain), Who provides bounty, who enriches human habitations, Who gives wheat, and brings grain into being.
        (27) Enbilulu-Hegal, who accumulates abundance for the peoples . . . . Who rains down riches on the broad earth, and supplies abundant vegetation.
        (28) Sirsir, who heaped up a mountain on top of Tiamat, Who plundered the corpse of Tiamat with [his] weapons, The guardian of the land, their trustworthy shepherd, Whose hair is a growing crop, whose turban is a furrow, Who kept crossing the broad Sea in his fury, And kept crossing over the place of her battle as though it were a bridge.
        (29) Sirsir-Malah they named him secondlyâ€”so be itâ€” Tiamat was his boat, he was her sailor.
        (30) Gil, who ever heaps up piles of barley, massive mounds, The creator of grain and flocks, who gives seed for the land.
        (31) Gilima, who made the bond of the gods firm, who created stability, A snare that overwhelmed them, who yet extended favours.
        (32) Agilima, the lofty, who snatches off the crown, who takes charge of snow, Who created the earth on the water and made firm the height of heaven.
        (33) Zulum, who assigns meadows for the gods and divides up what he has created, Who gives incomes and food-offerings, who administers shrines.
        (34) Mummu, creator of heaven and underworld, who protects refugees, The god who purifies heaven and underworld, secondly Zulummu, In respect of whose strength none other among the gods can equal him.
        (35) GiÅ¡numunab, creator of all the peoples, who made the world regions, Who destroyed Tiamat's gods, and made peoples from part of them.
        (36) Lugalabdubur, the king who scattered the works of Tiamat, who uprooted her weapons, Whose foundation is secure on the "Fore and Aft".
        (37) Pagalguenna, foremost of all lords, whose strength is exalted, Who is the greatest among the gods, his brothers, the most noble of them all.
        (38) Lugaldurmah, king of the bond of the gods, lord of Durmahu, Who is the greatest in the royal abode, infinitely more lofty than the other gods.
        (39) Aranunna, counsellor of Ea/Enki, creator of the gods, his fathers, Whom no god can equal in respect of his lordly walk.
        (40) Dumuduku, who renews for himself his pure abode in Duku, Dumuduku, without whom Lugalduku does not make a decision.
        (41) LugalÅ¡uanna, the king whose strength is exalted among the gods, The lord, the strength of Anu, he who is supreme, chosen of AnÅ¡ar.
        (42) Irugga, who plundered them all in the Sea, Who grasps all wisdom, is comprehensive in understanding.
        (43) Irqingu, who plundered Kingu in . . . battle, Who directs all decrees and establishes lordship.
        (44) Kinma, the director of all the gods, who gives counsel, At whose name the gods bend down in reverence as before a hurricane.
        (45) Dingir-Esiskurâ€”let him take his lofty seat in the House of Benediction, Let the gods bring their presents before him Until he receives their offerings. No one but he accomplishes clever things The four (regions) of black-heads are his creation, Apart from him no god knows the measure of their days.
        (46) Girru, who makes weapons hard (?), Who accomplished clever things in the battle with Tiamat, Comprehensive in wisdom, skilled in understanding, A deep mind, that all the gods combined do not understand.
        Let (47) Addu be his name, let him cover the whole span of heaven, Let him thunder with his pleasant voice upon the earth, May the rumble fill (?) the clouds And give sustenance to the peoples below.
        (48) AÅ¡a-ru, who, as his name says, mustered the Divine Fates He indeed is the warden of absolutely all peoples.
        As (49) Ne-beru let him hold the crossing place of heaven and underworld, They should not cross above or below, but should wait for him. Ne-beru is his star, which he caused to shine in the sky, Let him take his stand on the heavenly staircase that they may look at him. Yes, he who constantly crosses the Sea without resting, Let his name be Ne-beru, who grasps her middle, Let him fix the paths of the stars of heaven, Let him shepherd all the gods like sheep, Let him bind Tiamat and put her life in mortal danger, To generations yet unborn, to distant future days, May he continue unchecked, may he persist into eternity. Since he created the heavens and fashioned the earth,
        Enlil, the father, called him by his own name, (50) 'Lord of the Lands'.
        Ea/Enki heard the names which all the Igigi called And his spirit became radiant. "Why! He whose name was extolled by his fathers Let him, like me, be called (51) 'Ea/Enki'. Let him control the sum of all my rites, Let him administer all my decrees."
        With the word "Fifty" the great gods Called his fifty names and assigned him an outstanding position. They should be remembered; a leading figure should expound them, The wise and learned should confer about them, A father should repeat them and teach them to his son, One should explain them to shepherd and herdsman. If one is not negligent to Marduk, the Enlil of the gods, May one's land flourish, and oneself prosper, (For) his word is reliable, his command unchanged, No god can alter the utterance of his mouth. When he looks in fury, he does not relent, When his anger is ablaze, no god can face him. His mind is deep, his spirit is all-embracing, Before whom sin and transgression are sought out. Instruction which a leading figure repeated before him (Marduk): He wrote it down and stored it so that generations to come might hear it. [ . . ] . Marduk, who created the Igigi gods, Though they diminish . . . let them call on his name. . . . . the song of Marduk, Who defeated Tiamat and took kingship.
      The Golden Age
        The golden age came first, which, with no avengers, cultivated faith and right by its own free will without law. Aurea prima sata est aetas, quae vindice nullo,
        sponte sua, sine lege fidem rectumque colebat.         Punishment and fear were absent, nor were threatening words fixed on brass, poena metusque aberant, nec verba minantia fixo
        aere legebantur,         nor the begging throngs feared the oversight of judges, and they were safe without vigilantes. nec supplex turba timebat
        iudicis ora sui, sed erant sine vindice tuti.         Pines felled were not yet descended down their mountains into the liquid waves, nondum caesa suis, peregrinum ut viseret orbem,
        montibus in liquidas pinus descenderat undas,         and mortals knew no shores except their own. nullaque mortales praeter sua litora norant; Nor did rain-filled moats surround the towns. nondum praecipites cingebant oppida fossae; There were no trumpets of straight, nor horns bended brass, non tuba derecti, non aeris cornua flexi, nor helmets nor swords: the nations secure spent soft leisure without the use of soldiers. non galeae, non ensis erat: sine militis usu
        mollia securae peragebant otia gentes.         Terra, Herself also free and untouched by the harrow, nor wounded by any ploughshares, gave all things of itself. ipsa quoque inmunis rastroque intacta nec ullis
        saucia vomeribus per se dabat omnia tellus,         All were living in peace as Terra gave her fruits without cultivation or labor. In pace omnia vÄ«xerit quod Terra dedit fructus suum sine cultum vel labor. Being content, Terra created food without constraint, culled fruit and mountainous strawberries and cornels and blackberries sticking on harsh brambles and acorns which had fallen from the spreading tree of Saturn. Terra erat contenta
        cibis nullo cogente creatis
        arbuteos fetus montanaque fraga legebant
        cornaque et in duris haerentia mora rubetis
        et quae deciderant patula Saturni genitive of Saturn arbore glandes.         There was an everlasting spring, and gentle zephyrs fanned with warm breezes the flowers born without seeds. ver erat aeternum, placidique tepentibus auris
        mulcebant zephyri natos sine semine flores;         Soon also the Earth unploughed bore fruits and with no renovation the fields whitened with heavy ears of corn. mox etiam fruges tellus inarata ferebat,
        nec renovatus ager gravidis canebat aristis;         Now rivers of milk, now rivers of nectar were running, and yellow honies distilled from the verdant holm oak. flumina iam lactis, iam flumina nectaris ibant,
        flavaque de viridi stillabant ilice mella.         And so the world was in a Golden Age for five hundred years. Et sic mundus esset apud aetas auream quingenti annis.
      The Silver Age
        A silver race succeeded, worse than gold, more precious than tawny brass. subiit argentea proles, auro deterior, fulvo pretiosior aere. Saturn contracted the times of the ancient spring and drew out the year, in four spaces, through winters and summers, and unequal autumns, and a short spring. Saturnus antiqui contraxit tempora veris perque hiemes aestusque et inaequalis autumnos et breve ver spatiis exegit quattuor annum. Then first the air scorched with dry heats glowed and ice hung bound up by the winds. tum primum siccis aer fervoribus ustus canduit, et ventis glacies adstricta pependit; Then first men entered houses: caves and thick shrubs, and twigs bound with bark were their habitation. tum primum subiere domos; domus antra fuerunt et densi frutices et vinctae cortice virgae. Then first seeds of Ceres were buried in long furrows, and oxen bellowed, pressed by the yoke. semina tum primum longis Cerealia sulcis obruta sunt, pressique iugo gemuere iuvenci.

And the Goddess bright-eyed Minerva girded and clothed her with silvery raiment, and down from her head she spread with her hands a broidered veil, a wonder to see; and she, Pallas Minerva, put about her head lovely garlands, flowers of new-grown herbs. Also she put upon her head a crown of gold which the very famous Limping God made Himself and worked with His own hands as a favour to ğ’€­Jupiter His father. On it was much curious work, wonderful to see; for of the many creatures which the land and sea rear up, He put most upon it, wonderful things, like living beings with voices: and great beauty shone out from it. And as in thatched hives bees feed the drones whose nature is to do mischief â€” by day and throughout the day until the sun goes down the bees are busy and lay the white combs, while the drones stay at home in the covered skeps and reap the toil of others into their own bellies â€” even so ğ’€­Jupiter who thunders on high made women to be an evil to mortal men, with a nature to do evil. And he gave them a second evil to be the price for the good they had: whoever avoids marriage and the sorrows that women cause, and will not wed, reaches deadly old age without anyone to tend his years, and though he at least has no lack of livelihood while he lives, yet, when he is dead, his kinsfolk divide his possessions amongst them. And as for the man who chooses the lot of marriage and takes a good wife suited to his mind, evil continually contends with good; for whoever happens to have mischievous children, lives always with unceasing grief in his spirit and heart within him; and this evil cannot be healed. á½©Ï‚ Î´á¾½ postpositional but á½Ï€á½¹Ï„á¾½ when á¼Î½ among ÏƒÎ¼á½µÎ½ÎµÏƒÏƒÎ¹ ÎºÎ±Ï„Î·ÏÎµÏ†á½³ÎµÏƒÏƒÎ¹ Î¼á½³Î»Î¹ÏƒÏƒÎ±Î¹

        Nona
        ÎšÎ»Ï‰Î¸á½½Klotho
        One of the three Fates, or personifications of destiny, and Roman Goddess of pregnancy.
        She spins the thread of life.
        Her name means "ninth" for she is called upon by pregnant women in their ninth month when the child is due to be born.

      The Iron Age
        The last age is of hard iron, and deadly Night bare Nemesis to afflict mortal men. de duro est ultima ferro, Î¤á½·ÎºÏ„Îµ gave birth Î´á½² postpositional but ÎºÎ±á½¶ and Îá½³Î¼ÎµÏƒÎ¹Î½ accusative Nemesis, Ï€á¿†Î¼Î± misery Î¸Î½Î·Ï„Î¿á¿–ÏƒÎ¹ mortal Î²ÏÎ¿Ï„Î¿á¿–ÏƒÎ¹ dative mortal men, human beings, Îá½ºÎ¾ f Nox; night á½€Î»Î¿á½µ destructive, deadlyÎ‡ And last She bore obstinate-souled Strife.         navita, quaeque prius steterant in montibus altis,
        fluctibus ignotis insultavere carinae,         And the cautious surveyors marked with long boundaries the ground, which before was as free as the light of the sun or as the air. communemque prius ceu lumina solis et auras
        cautus humum longo signavit limite mensor.         And the rich ground was asked to provide more than merely crops and nourishment, and men went into the bowels of the earth to dig up the riches (the incentives of evil) which had been hidden in the infernal deep caves. nec tantum segetes alimentaque debita dives

        effodiuntur opes, inritamenta malorum.         And now hurtful iron and gold (more hurtful than iron) had come forth; and War came forth, which uses both, and shakes together rattling arms with bloody hand. iamque nocens ferrum ferroque nocentius aurum

        sanguineaque manu crepitantia concutit arma. The guest was not protected from his host, nor the father-in-law from his own son-in-law; even brothers seldom could abide in peace. non hospes ab hospite tutus,
        non socer a genero, fratrum quoque gratia rara est;
         The husband threatened to destroy his wife, and she her husband; horrid step-mothers mixed lurid poisons; eager sons inquired their fathers' age before their appointed day; inminet exitio vir coniugis, illa mariti,
        lurida terribiles miscent aconita novercae,
        filius ante before diem patrios inquirit in annos:         Piety lies vanquished; and the virgin Astraea is the last of the celestials to leave the blood-soaked earth. victa iacet pietas, et virgo caede madentis
        ultima caelestum terras Astraea reliquit.
         And so the people of the world lived in an Iron Age for five hundred years. Et sic mundus esset apud aetas ferro quingenti annis.

      Gigantomachy

      Gigantomachy
        And lest the higher aether should long remain less troubled than the earth, it is recorded that the Giants aspired to the celestial kingdom, and built up mountains heaped together to the lofty stars. Neve foret terris securior arduus aether,
        adfectasse ferunt regnum caeleste gigantas
        altaque congestos struxisse ad sidera montis.
        Ossa
        a mountain in Thessaly, Greece, southeast of Olympus
        Pelion
        a mountain in southeastern Thessaly, Greece, southeast of Ossa

        The Giants had piled Pelion onto Ossa to attack the Gods in Olympus

        Then the all-powerful Father broke through Olympus with his hurled thunderbolt, and threw Mount Pelion down from Ossa below it. tum pater omnipotens misso perfregit Olympum
        fulmine et excussit subiecto Pelion Ossae.         When their dead bodies lay overwhelmed by their own mass, they tell that the earth, over sprinkled with much blood of her children, grew moist, and animated the warm gore: lest no monuments of the savage stock should remain, converted it into the form of men. obruta mole sua cum corpora dira iacerent,
        perfusam multo natorum sanguine Terram
        immaduisse ferunt calidumque animasse cruorem
        et, ne nulla suae stirpis monimenta manerent,
        in faciem vertisse hominum;         But also that progeny was a scorner of the Gods above, and most greedy of cruel slaughter, and violent. You would know they were born from blood. sed et illa propago
        contemptrix superum saevaeque avidissima caedis
        et violenta fuit: scires e sanguine natos.         When Father Saturn saw this from His high throne, He groans. Quae pater ut summa vidit Saturnius arce,
        ingemit.
      The DelugeAt first Saturn would call upon Lua Saturni to bring down destruction upon the earth, but He feared, once set free on Her own, that She would overwhelm even the whole universe. A different punishment seems good, to destroy the mortal race under the waters, and to send down rains from all the sky. Luam Saturni invocaret primo deducere exitium
      terrae qui autem timore perterritus, totum semel
      set liberum sui est, eam universum disperdat.
      poena placet diversa, genus mortale sub undis
      perdere et ex omni nimbos demittere caelo.       Immediately He closes in Aeolian caves the Northwind and every other wind that might dispel the gathering clouds and sends forth the Southwind. Protinus Aeoliis Aquilonem claudit in antris
      et quaecumque fugant inductas flamina nubes
      emittitque Notum.       Auster the south wind flies forth on moist wings, concealing in the mist his awful face;
      madidis Notus evolat alis,
      terribilem picea tectus caligine vultum;       the drenching rain descends from his beard and waves flow from his white hair; barba gravis nimbis, canis fluit unda capillis; mists sit upon his forehead, and his feathers and folds drop dew. fronte sedent nebulae, rorant pennaeque sinusque. And when he pressed the hanging clouds with his broad hand, a crash is made: hence heavy showers are poured from the sky. utque manu lata pendentia nubila pressit,
      fit fragor: hinc densi funduntur ab aethere nimbi;       Iris Rainbow, the messanger of Ops, arrayed in various colors, collects waters and brings supplies to the clouds. nuntia Opis varios induta colores
      concipit Iris aquas alimentaque nubibus adfert.       The crops are laid low and the hopes of the farmers lie lamenting, and the ineffectual labor of the long year perishes. sternuntur segetes et deplorata coloni
      vota iacent, longique perit labor inritus anni.       Nor was the anger of Saturn content with His own sky, His own azure brother helps him with aiding waters. Nec caelo contenta suo est Saturni ira, sed illum
      caeruleus frater iuvat auxiliaribus undis.       Oceanus calls together the rivers, who when they entered the roof of their king, He says â€œA long appeal is needless; bring forth in rage of powers, open your fountains, rush over obstacles, let every stream pour forth in boundless floods,â€ He had commanded. convocat hic amnes: qui postquam tecta tyranni
      intravere sui, 'non est hortamine longo
      nunc' ait 'utendum; vires effundite vestras:
      sic opus est! aperite domos ac mole remota
      fluminibus vestris totas inmittite habenas!'
      iusserat;       The servants return and let loose mouths to their fountains and roll with unbridled course into the sea. hi redeunt ac fontibus ora relaxant
      et defrenato volvuntur in aequora cursu.       And He strikes the earth with his trident, which trembles and lay open its bosom of water with the shock. Ipse tridente suo terram percussit, at illa
      intremuit motuque vias patefecit aquarum.       The Rivers spreading rush through the open plains and snatch away groves together with sown wheat, and flocks, and men, and dwellings, and sanctuaries with their sacred things. exspatiata ruunt per apertos flumina campos
      cumque satis arbusta simul pecudesque virosque
      tectaque cumque suis rapiunt penetralia sacris.       If any house remained and undemolished was able to resist so great an evil, nevertheless the wave, being higher, covers its top and its tower pressed down totter under the eddy. si qua domus mansit potuitque resistere tanto
      indeiecta malo, culmen tamen altior huius
      unda tegit, pressaeque latent sub gurgite turres.       And now the sea and land had no distinction; all things were a sea. iamque mare et tellus nullum discrimen habebant:
      omnia pontus erat,       Shores also were wanting to the sea. derant quoque litora ponto. One man occupies a hill; another sits in a hollow boat, and there draws his oars where he had always ploughed. Occupat hic collem, cumba sedet alter adunca
      et ducit remos illic, ubi nuper arabat:       That man sails above corn crops or the roofs of a sunken villa; this catches a fish on the top of an elm. ille supra segetes aut mersae culmina villae
      navigat, hic summa piscem deprendit in ulmo.       The anchor is fixed in a green meadow, if chance has directed it, figitur in viridi, si fors tulit, ancora prato, or curved keels rubs against vineyards placed under them; aut subiecta terunt curvae vineta carinae; and where just now slender she-goats cropped the grass, there now misshapen sea-calves place their bodies. et, modo qua graciles gramen carpsere capellae,
      nunc ibi deformes ponunt sua corpora phocae.       The Nereids admire groves and cities and houses under the water. mirantur sub aqua lucos urbesque domosque
      Nereides, silvasque tenent delphines et altis       And dolphins possess woods, course over the high boughs, and beat the shaken oaks. silvasque tenent delphines et altis
      incursant ramis agitataque robora pulsant.       The wolf swims among the sheep, nat lupus inter oves, the wave carries tawny lions, fulvos vehit unda leones, the wave carries tigers. unda vehit tigres; Nor do his powers of lightning avail the boar nor his swift legs the stag carried away. nec vires fulminis apro,
      crura nec ablato prosunt velocia cervo,       And having sought for land a long time, where it might be granted to settle, the wandering bird with wearied wings, falls down into the sea. quaesitisque diu terris, ubi sistere possit,
      in mare lassatis volucris vaga decidit alis.       The unbounded license of the ocean had overwhelmed the hillocks and new waves were beating the mountain tops. obruerat tumulos inmensa licentia ponti,
      pulsabantque novi montana cacumina fluctus.       The greatest part is hurried away by the wave; the long fastings subdue by scanty food those whom the water has spared. maxima pars unda rapitur; quibus unda pepercit,
      illos longa domant inopi ieiunia victu.       Phocis, a rich land, while it was land, separates the Aonians from the Aetolian fields, but in that time it was a part of the sea and a broad plain of sudden waters. Separat Aonios Oetaeis Phocis ab arvis,
      terra ferax, dum terra fuit, sed tempore in illo
      pars maris et latus subitarum campus aquarum.       There a lofty mountain, Parnassus by name, seeks the stars with two peaks, and surmounts the clouds with its top. mons ibi verticibus petit arduus astra duobus,
      nomine Parnasos, superantque cacumina nubes.       When Deucalion, carried on a small raft with the partner of his bed, stuck here (for the sea had covered the rest), they adore the Corycian nymphs and the Deities of the mountain, and the prophetic Themis, who then held the oracle. hic ubi Deucalion (nam cetera texerat aequor)
      cum consorte tori parva rate raft vectus adhaesit,
      Corycidas nymphas et numina montis adorant
      fatidicamque Themin, quae tunc oracla tenebat:       There was not any man better than he, nor more loving of equity; or any woman more fearing of the Gods than she. non illo melior quisquam nec amantior aequi
      vir fuit aut illa metuentior ulla deorum.       When Saturn sees that the world lies stagnant with liquid pools and that one man alone survives from so many thousands just now; and sees that one woman survives of so many thousands just now; innocent, both worshippers of the Deity; He dispersed the clouds, and the showers having been removed by the Northwind, He showed both the lands to the sky, and the aether to the lands. Saturnus ut liquidis stagnare paludibus orbem
      et superesse virum de tot modo milibus unum,
      et superesse vidit de tot modo milibus unam,
      innocuos ambo, cultores numinis ambo,
      nubila disiecit nimbisque aquilone remotis
      et caelo terras ostendit et aethera terris.       Nor does the anger of the sea remain, and the Ruler of the ocean soothes the waters, his three-pointed weapon being laid aside, and he calls caerulean Triton standing up above the deep, and covered as to his shoulders with native purple; and He orders Him to breathe into his sounding shell, and already to recall the waves and the floods at a given signal. nec maris ira manet, positoque tricuspide telo
      mulcet aquas rector pelagi supraque profundum
      exstantem atque umeros innato murice tectum
      caeruleum Tritona vocat conchaeque sonanti
      inspirare iubet fluctusque et flumina signo
      iam revocare dato:       The hollow-wreathed bugle-horn, which increases from the lowest bend into a broad top, is taken by Him, cava bucina sumitur illi,
      tortilis in latum quae turbine crescit ab imo,       the bugle, which, when it has collected air in the midst of the sea, fills with its voice the shores lying under either sun. bucina, quae medio concepit ubi aera ponto,
      litora voce replet sub utroque iacentia Phoebo;       Then also when it touched the lips of the God, dropping dew from his moist beard, and being blown, sounded the commanded retreat of the waters, it was heard by all the waters of the earth and of the sea, tum quoque, ut ora dei madida rorantia barba
      contigit et cecinit iussos inflata receptus,
      omnibus audita est telluris et aequoris undis,       and by whatever waters it was heard, it restrained all of them. et quibus est undis audita, coercuit omnes. Already the sea has a shore, their channel contains the full rivers, iam mare litus habet, plenos capit alveus amnes, the floods sink down andÂ the hills are seen to come forth, flumina subsidunt collesque exire videntur; the ground rises, grounds increase by the waters decreasing, surgit humus, crescunt sola decrescentibus undis, and after a long day the woods show their tops naked and hold slime left on their foliage. postque diem longam nudata cacumina silvae
      ostendunt limumque tenent in fronde relictum
      Repopulating the World
        The globe was restored; which when Deucalion saw empty, and that the desolated lands kept deep silence, he addresses Pyrrha thus, with gushing tears: Redditus orbis erat; quem postquam vidit inanem
        et desolatas agere alta silentia terras,
        Deucalion lacrimis ita Pyrrham adfatur obortis:         â€œO my sister! O spouse! O woman, sole surviving, whom a common race and kindred origin, and then the marriage bed, hath joined to me; and now themselves join. We two are the crowd of all the lands, whatever the setting and the rising look upon. The sea has taken possession of the rest. 'o soror, o coniunx, o femina sola superstes,
        quam commune mihi genus et patruelis origo,
        deinde torus iunxit, nunc ipsa pericula iungunt,
        terrarum, quascumque vident occasus et ortus,
        nos duo turba sumus; possedit cetera pontus.         â€œnow also confidence in our life is not as yet sufficiently certain: even now the clouds terrify my mind. haec quoque adhuc vitae non est fiducia nostrae
        certa satis; terrent etiamnum nubila mentem.         â€œO you to be pitied, what feeling would arise now to you if you had been snatched from the fates without me? quis tibi, si sine me fatis erepta fuisses,
        nunc animus, miseranda, foret?         â€œIn what manner could you bear your fear alone? Who consoling could you bear your sorrows? quo sola timorem
        ferre modo posses? quo consolante doleres!         â€œFor I, (believe me), if only the sea had you, would follow you, O spouse, and the sea should have me likewise. namque ego (crede mihi), si te quoque pontus haberet,
        te sequerer, coniunx, et me quoque pontus haberet.         â€œO I wish that I were able to repair the nations by my fatherâ€™s arts, and to infuse souls into the molded earth; now the mortal race rests in us two; thus it hath seemed to the gods above, and we remain patterns of human beings.â€ o utinam possim populos reparare paternis
        artibus atque animas formatae infundere terrae!
        nunc genus in nobis restat mortale duobus.
        sic visum superis: hominumque exempla manemus.'         He had spoken, and they wept. It seemed good to pray to the celestial divinity; and to seek aid by sacred lots. dixerat, et flebant: placuit caeleste precari
        numen et auxilium per sacras quaerere sortes.         There is no delay: together they go to the waters of the Cephisus as not yet clear so already cutting known channels. nulla mora est: adeunt pariter Cephesidas undas,
        ut nondum liquidas, sic iam vada nota secantes.         Thence, when they had sprinkled the waters tasted on their vests, and head, they bend their footsteps to the temples of the sacred Goddess; inde ubi libatos inroravere liquores
        vestibus et capiti, flectunt vestigia sanctae
        ad delubra deae,         the tops of which were defiled with foul moss, and the altars were standing without fires. quorum fastigia turpi
        pallebant musco stabantque sine ignibus arae.         When they touched the steps of the temple, each prostrate on the ground, and gave kisses to the icy stone. ut templi tetigere gradus, procumbit uterque
        pronus humi gelidoque pavens dedit oscula saxo         And thus they said; â€œO Themis, if the deities relent overcome by just prayers, if the anger of the gods is turned, say by what are the loss of our race may be reparable, and, most gentle goddess, bring assistance to our sunken fortunes.â€ atque ita 'si precibus' dixerunt 'numina iustis
        victa remollescunt, si flectitur ira deorum,
        dic, Themi, qua generis damnum reparabile nostri
        arte sit, et mersis fer opem, mitissima, rebus!'         The goddess was moved, and gave the oracle: â€œDepart from the temple, and veil the head, and loose your girded garments, and cast the bones of your great parent behind your back.â€ Mota dea est sortemque dedit: 'discedite templo
        et velate caput cinctasque resolvite vestes
        ossaque post tergum magnae iactate parentis!'         They were astounded a long time and Pyrrha first breaks silence with her voice, and refuses to obey the commands of the goddess; and asks with trembling mouth that she will give excuse to her; and fears to offend her motherâ€™s shades, by casting her bones. obstupuere diu: rumpitque silentia voce
        Pyrrha prior iussisque deae parere recusat,
        detque sibi veniam pavido rogat ore pavetque
        laedere iactatis maternas ossibus umbras.         Meanwhile they repeat with themselves the words of the given oracle, obscure withdark mysteries, and revolve them between themselves. interea repetunt caecis obscura latebris
        verba datae sortis secum inter seque volutant.         After the son of Prometheus soothes the daughter of Epimetheus with gentle words, and says â€œAiii either there is some deceitful subtlety against us, or the oracles are pious, and advise no unrighteousness. inde Promethides placidis Epimethida dictis
        mulcet et 'aut fallax' ait 'est sollertia nobis,
        aut (pia sunt nullumque nefas oracula suadent!)         â€œThe earth our great parent; I suppose that the stones in the body of the earth are called bones; we are ordered to cast these behind our back.â€ magna parens terra est: lapides in corpore terrae
        ossa reor dici; iacere hos post terga iubemur.'         Although the daughter of Titan was moved by the interpretation of her husband, nevertheless hope is in in doubt. Both so distrust the heavenly instructions. But what harm will it do to try? Coniugis augurio quamquam Titania mota est,
        spes tamen in dubio est: adeo caelestibus ambo
        diffidunt monitis; sed quid temptare nocebit?         They descend and cover the head, and ungird their tunics; and throw the stones as commanded behind their footsteps. descendunt: velantque caput tunicasque recingunt
        et iussos lapides sua post vestigia mittunt.         The stones (who can credit this, unless antiquity be as a witness?) began to lay aside their hardness, and their stiffness, and to bo softened by delay, and being softened to assume a form. saxa (quis hoc credat, nisi sit pro teste vetustas?)
        ponere duritiem coepere suumque rigorem
        mollirique mora mollitaque ducere formam.         Presently, when they increased, and a gentler nature had accrued to them, as some form of man may be seen yet so it is not clear; mox ubi creverunt naturaque mitior illis
        contigit, ut quaedam, sic non manifesta videri
        forma potest hominis,         but as begun from marble not sufficiently finished, and similar to rough statues. sed uti de marmore coepta
        non exacta satis rudibusque simillima signis,         Nevertheless the part of them which was moist with any juice, and earthy, was turned to the use of the body; quae tamen ex illis aliquo pars umida suco
        et terrena fuit, versa est in corporis usum;         that which is solid, and cannot be bent, is changed into bones; quod solidum est flectique nequit, mutatur in ossa, That which just now was vein, remained under the same name. quae modo vena fuit, sub eodem nomine mansit, And in a brief space, by the gift of the Gods above, 'the stones thrown by the hands of the man derived a manly appearance, and woman was created from the woman's casting, inque brevi spatio superorum numine saxa
        missa viri manibus faciem traxere virorum
        et de femineo reparata est femina iactu.         thence we are sprung, a hard race, and having trial of labours, and we give proofs from what origin we were born. inde genus durum sumus experiensque laborum
        et documenta damus qua simus origine nati.

          The Old Kingdom of Egypt
            Apollo did wish for Egypt to prosper And so He gave the remains of the Python to the people of Egypt as the River Nile For five hundred years the people of Egypt owed their lifes to the River Nile Many men fought for control of the River It was Menes, Son of Sol, to unite all of Egypt And so was formed the Old Kingdom of Egypt

          Middle Kingdom of Egypt
            The Pharaohs of Egypt ruled with iron fists They constructed grand buildings to honor only themselves Only the Pharaoh benefited from the labor of the people of Egypt The Gods led the noblemen to revolt Egypt fell into anarchy Noblemen had to cultivate land to survive Relative turned against relative To resolve the fighting, Sol gave half of Egypt to each His two sons Order was restored within their separate realms Each half grew jealous of the other War between the Upper and Lower Egypts erupted in time Upper Egypt conquered Lower Egypt Peace was restored to all of Egypt Pharaohs again had control People demanded better care from the Pharaohs And so was formed the Middle Kingdom of Egypt
          Birth of ğ’€­Jupiter
            In the chamber of the Destinies, the room of the archetypes, The wisest of the wise, the sage of the Gods, ğ’€­Jupiter was conceived. In Abzu was ğ’€­Jupiter born, In pure Abzu was ğ’€­Jupiter born. Ea/Enki his Father begat Him, Damkina his Mother bore Him. He sucked the breasts of Goddesses, A nurse reared Him and filled Him with terror. His figure was well developed, the glance of His eyes was dazzling, His growth was manly, He was mighty from the beginning. Caelus, His Father's begetter, saw Him, He exulted and smiled; His heart filled with joy. Caelus rendered Him perfect: His divinity was remarkable, And He became very lofty, excelling Them in His attributes. His members were incomprehensibly wonderful, Incapable of being grasped with the mind, hard even to look on. Four were His eyes, four His ears, Flame shot forth as He moved His lips. His four ears grew large, And His eyes likewise took in everything. His figure was lofty and superior in comparison with the Gods, His limbs were surpassing, His nature was superior. â€œMari-utu, Mari-utu, â€œThe Son, the Sun-God, the Sun-God of the Gods.â€ He was clothed with the aura of the Ten Gods, so exalted was His strength, The Fifty Dreads were loaded upon him.

            FRAGMENT 3 - AEGAEON
Scholiast on Apollonius Rhodius, Arg. i. 1165:
Eumelus says that Aegaeon was the son of Earth and Sea and, having his dwelling in the sea, was an ally of the Titans.

            FRAGMENT 6 - SATURN AND PHILYRA
Scholiast on Apollonius Rhodius, Arg. i. 554:
The author of the War of the Giants says that Saturn took the shape of a horse and lay with Philyra, the daughter of Ocean. Through this cause Chiron was born a centaur: his wife was Chariclo.

            FRAGMENT 7 - HERCULES
Athenaeus, xi. 470 B:
Theolytus says that he (Hercules) sailed across the sea in a cauldron; but the first to give this story is the author of the War of the Titans.
          Fragments of Titanomachy, translated by H. G. Evelyn-White
          Titanomachy

So He said. á½­Ï‚ Ï†á½±Ï„á¾½Î‡ And the Gods, givers of good things, applauded when They heard His word, and Their spirit longed for war even more than before, and They all, both male and female, stirred up hated battle that day, the Titan Gods, and all that were born of Saturn together with those dread, mighty ones of overwhelming strength whom ğ’€­Jupiter brought up to the light from Darkness beneath the earth. An hundred arms sprang from the shoulders of all alike, and each had fifty heads growing upon His shoulders upon stout limbs. These, then, stood against the Titans in grim strife, holding huge rocks in their strong hands. And on the other part the Titans eagerly strengthened Their ranks, and both sides at one time showed the work of Their hands and Their might. The boundless sea rang terribly around, and the earth crashed loudly: wide Heaven was shaken and groaned, and high Olympus reeled from its foundation under the charge of the undying Gods, and a heavy quaking reached dim Tartarus and the deep sound of Their feet in the fearful onset and of Their hard missiles.             ÎºÎµÎºÎ»Î¿Î¼á½³Î½Ï‰Î½Î‡ Î¿á¼³ Î´á½² postpositional but Î¾á½»Î½Î¹ÏƒÎ±Î½ Î¼ÎµÎ³á½±Î»á¿³ big, large; mighty; marvelous á¼€Î»Î±Î»Î·Ï„á¿·.             Then ğ’€­Jupiter no longer held back His might; but straight His heart was filled with fury and He showed forth all His strength. From Heaven and from Olympus He came forthwith, hurling His lightning: the bold flew thick and fast from His strong hand together with thunder and lightning, whirling an awesome flame. The life-giving earth crashed around in burning, and the vast wood crackled loud with fire all about. All the land seethed, and Ocean's streams and the unfruitful sea. The hot vapour lapped round the earthborn Titans: flame unspeakable rose to the bright upper air: the flashing glare of the thunder-stone and lightning blinded Their eyes for all that there were strong. Astounding heat seized Chaos: and to see with eyes and to hear the sound with ears it seemed even as if Earth and wide Heaven above came together; for such a mighty crash would have arisen if Earth were being hurled to ruin, and Heaven from on high were hurling her down; so great a crash was there while the Gods were meeting together in strife. Also the winds brought rumbling earthquake and duststorm, thunder and lightning and the lurid thunderbolt, which are the shafts of great ğ’€­Jupiter, and carried the clangour and the warcry into the midst of the two hosts. A horrible uproar of terrible strife arose: mighty deeds were shown and the battle inclined. But until then, They kept at one another and fought continually in cruel war.           Theogony
            And amongst the foremost Cottus and Briareus and Gyges insatiate for war raised fierce fighting: three hundred rocks, one upon another, they launched from their strong hands and overshadowed the Titans with their missiles, and buried them beneath the wide-pathed earth, and bound them in bitter chains when they had conquered them by their strength for all their great spirit, as far beneath the earth to Tartarus. For a brazen anvil falling down from heaven nine nights and days would reach the earth upon the tenth: and again, a brazen anvil falling from earth nine nights and days would reach Tartarus upon the tenth. Round it runs a fence of bronze, and night spreads in triple line all about it like a neck-circlet, while above grow the roots of the earth and unfruitful sea. There by the counsel of ğ’€­Jupiter who drives the clouds the Titan gods are hidden under misty gloom, in a dank place where are the ends of the huge earth. And they may not go out; for Neptune fixed gates of bronze upon it, and a wall runs all round it on every side.             á½¨ÎºÎµÎ±Î½Î¿á¿–Î¿ genitive Oceanus Îºá½³ÏÎ±Ï‚ horn; arm or branch of a riverÎ‡             With nine silver-swirling streams He winds about the earth and the sea's wide back, and then falls into the main (24); but the tenth flows out from a rock, a sore trouble to the Gods. For whoever of the deathless Gods that hold the peaks of snowy Olympus pours a libation of Her water is forsworn, lies breathless until a full year is completed, and never comes near to taste ambrosia and nectar, but lies spiritless and voiceless on a strewn bed: and a heavy trance overshadows Him. But when He has spent a long year in His sickness, another penance and a harder follows after the first. For nine years He is cut off from the eternal Gods and never joins Their councils of Their feasts, nine full years. But in the tenth year He comes again to join the assemblies of the deathless Gods who live in the house of Olympus. Such an oath, then, did the Gods appoint the eternal and primeval water of Styx to be: and it spouts through a rugged place. Î¿á½— his Ï‡Îµá¿–ÏÎµÏ‚ hand Î¼á½²Î½ á¼”Î±ÏƒÎ¹Î½ á¼Ï€á¾½ on á¼°ÏƒÏ‡á½»Î¹, á¼”ÏÎ³Î¼Î±Ï„á¾½ á¼”Ï‡Î¿Ï…ÏƒÎ±Î¹,
            ÎºÎ±á½¶ also Ï€á½¹Î´ÎµÏ‚ á¼€Îºá½±Î¼Î±Ï„Î¿Î¹ ÎºÏÎ±Ï„ÎµÏÎ¿á¿¦ Î¸ÎµÎ¿á¿¦ genitive godÎ‡             From his shoulders grew an hundred heads of a snake, a fearful dragon, with dark, flickering tongues, and from under the brows of his eyes in his marvellous heads flashed fire, and fire burned from his heads as he glared. And there were voices in all his dreadful heads which uttered every kind of sound unspeakable; for at one time they made sounds such that the gods understood, but at another, the noise of a bull bellowing aloud in proud ungovernable fury; and at another, the sound of a lion, relentless of heart; and at anothers, sounds like whelps, wonderful to hear; and again, at another, he would hiss, so that the high mountains re-echoed. And truly a thing past help would have happened on that day, and he would have come to reign over mortals and immortals, had not the father of men and gods been quick to perceive it. But he thundered hard and mightily: and the earth around resounded terribly and the wide heaven above, and the sea and Ocean's streams and the nether parts of the earth. Great Olympus reeled beneath the divine feet of the king as he arose and earth groaned thereat. And through the two of them heat took hold on the dark-blue sea, through the thunder and lightning, and through the fire from the monster, and the scorching winds and blazing thunderbolt. The whole earth seethed, and sky and sea: and the long waves raged along the beaches round and about, at the rush of the deathless gods: and there arose an endless shaking. Hades trembled where he rules over the dead below, and the Titans under Tartarus who live with Saturn, because of the unending clamour and the fearful strife. So when ğ’€­Jupiter had raised up His might and seized his arms, thunder and lightning and lurid thunderbolt, he leaped from Olympus and struck him, and burned all the marvellous heads of the monster about Him. But when ğ’€­Jupiter had conquered him and lashed him with strokes, Typhoeus was hurled down, a maimed wreck, so that the huge earth groaned. And flame shot forth from the thunder-stricken lord in the dim rugged glens of the mount (26), when he was smitten. A great part of huge earth was scorched by the terrible vapour and melted as tin melts when heated by men's art in channelled (27) crucibles; or as iron, which is hardest of all things, is softened by glowing fire in mountain glens and melts in the divine earth through the strength of Vulcan (28). Even so, then, the earth melted in the glow of the blazing fire.             á¿¬á¿–ÏˆÎµ Î´á½³ postpositional but, and Î¼Î¹Î½ her Î¸Ï…Î¼á¿· dative soul; heart; desire á¼€ÎºÎ±Ï‡á½¼Î½ á¼Ï‚ into Î¤á½±ÏÏ„Î±ÏÎ¿Î½ accusative Tartarus Îµá½Ïá½»Î½.             And from Typhoeus come boisterous winds which blow damply, except Auster and Boreas and clear Zephyr. These are a god-sent kind, and a great blessing to men; but the others blow fitfully upon the seas. Some rush upon the misty sea and work great havoc among men with their evil, raging blasts; for varying with the season they blow, scattering ships and destroying sailors. And men who meet these upon the sea have no help against the mischief. Others again over the boundless, flowering earth spoil the fair fields of men who dwell below, filling them with dust and cruel uproar. á¼˜Îº from Î´á½² postpositional but Î¤Ï…Ï†Ï‰á½³Î¿Ï‚ á¼”ÏƒÏ„á¾½ to be, exist, live á¼€Î½á½³Î¼Ï‰Î½ genitive winds Î¼á½³Î½Î¿Ï‚ mind; courage; power, strength á½‘Î³Ïá½¸Î½ á¼€á½³Î½Ï„Ï‰Î½,

          Caelus Deceives the Canaanites
            The Hebrews were led by Abraham Caelus threatened Abraham and had the Hebrews worship no other Gods but Himself The Sons of Abraham did take heed of His words The Sons of Asshur lived now in Mesopotamia These Assyrians continued to worship the Gods from the tradition of the Sumerians There was much fighting between the Hebrews and the Assyrians Caelus promised the Hebrews more fertile land to the west, in the land of Canaan Abraham instead found the land to be under a drought Abraham agreed with Caelus to have all sons of him to be circumcised Caelus praised Abraham and made many promises to him
          Hammurabi and the First Babylonian Dynasty
            The Amorites worshipped Hadad (ğ’€­Jupiter) in the land of Canaan They moved into the central plains and founded the city of Babylon (First Babylonian Dynasty, 1894 BCE â€“ 1595 BCE) (Hammurabi, 1792 BCE â€“ 1750 BCE) Hammurabi freed Mesopotamia from Elamite rule Hammurabi removed the power of Enlil in Nippur and proclaimed Marduk as the Supreme God (Sack of Babylon by Hittites, 1595 BCE)
          Dialogue between a Man and His God
          New Kingdom of Egypt
            Egypt was full of power and wealth during this reign People flocked to the grand cities along the Nile Joseph, grandson of Isaac, foretold the coming of famine And famine did spread throughout Egypt Pharaoh Amenemhat invited the Sons of Jacob, son of Isaac, into Egypt Amenemhat lost control of Egypt as the famines continued Pharaoh Neferhotep allowed Egypt to splinter The Hyksos, Sons of Joseph gained control of each new vassal The Hyksos took control of Itj-tawy, honorary capital of Egypt The great city of Thebes in Lower Egypt declared itself the new proper capital of Egypt The leaders of Thebes slowly conquered the Hyksos Pharaoh Amose reclaimed all of Egypt under the rule of Thebes And so was formed the New Kingdom of Egypt
          Minoan Civilization
            In the sea of Neptune slept the island of Crete The Cretans of Crete were born from Pandora, wife of Epimetheus, Son of Iapetus They were led by the stern rule of Minos, Son of Pandora (Height of the Minoan Civilization) Minos begot Lycastus who begot Minos II (Discussion of the Minotaur) Minos II begot Deucalion who begot Idomeneus
          Mycenaean Greece
            (Mycenaean people take control over Greece)
          Phoenicians
            From the Amorites came the Phoenicians, who spread themselves across the Mediterranean Sea
          Ancient Greece
            (Chapter the Final) (The Greeks rise to power as a military and trading power)
